                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Citrus are widely consumed worldwide as juice or fresh fruit, providing important sources of vitamin C and other health-promoting compounds. Global production in 2012 exceeded 86 million metric tons, with an estimated value of $9 billion (<a href="http://www.fas.usda.gov/psdonline/circulars/citrus.pdf">http://www.fas.usda.gov/psdonline/circulars/citrus.pdf</a>). The very narrow genetic diversity of cultivated citrus makes them highly vulnerable to disease outbreaks, including citrus greening disease (also known as Huanglongbing or HLB), which is rapidly spreading throughout the world's major citrus-producing regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bové, J. Huanglongbing: a destructive, newly-emerging, century-old disease of citrus. J. Plant Pathol. 88, 7–37 (2006)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d9185e1401">1</a></sup>. Understanding the population genomics and domestication of citrus will enable strategies for improvements, including resistance to greening and other diseases.</p><p>The domestication and distribution of edible citrus types began several thousand years ago in Southeast Asia and spread globally, following ancient land and sea routes. The lineages that gave rise to most modern cultivated varieties, however, have been lost in undocumented antiquity, and their identities remain controversial<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="The Citrus Industry 1st edn, Vol. 1 (eds. Reuther, W., Webber, H.J. &amp; Batchelor, L.D.). (University of California, Division of Agricultural Sciences, Berkeley, California, USA, 1967)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d9185e1408">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Spiegel-Roy, P. &amp; Goldschmidt, E.E. Biology of citrus (Cambridge University Press, Cambridge and New York, 1996)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d9185e1411">3</a></sup>. Several features of <i>Citrus</i> biology and cultivation make deciphering these origins difficult. Cultivated varieties are typically propagated clonally by grafting and through asexual seed production (apomixis via nucellar polyembryony) to maintain desirable combinations of traits (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). Thus, many important cultivar groups have characteristic basic genotypes that presumably arose through interspecific hybridization and/or successive introgressive hybridizations of wild ancestral species. These domestication events predated the global expansion of citrus cultivation by hundreds or perhaps thousands of years, with no record of the domestication process. Diversity within such groups arises through accumulated somatic mutations, generally without sexual recombination, either as limb sports on trees or variants among apomictic seedling progeny.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="A selection of mandarin, pummelo and orange fruits, including cultivars sequenced in this study."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: A selection of mandarin, pummelo and orange fruits, including cultivars sequenced in this study.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2906/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2906/MediaObjects/41587_2014_Article_BFnbt2906_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2906/MediaObjects/41587_2014_Article_BFnbt2906_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1035"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Pummelos (1,2 in outline on left) are large trees that produce very large fruit with white, pink or red flesh color (2) and yellow or pink rinds. Most cultivars have large leaves with petioles with prominent wings. Apomictic reproduction is absent, and most selections are self-incompatible. Mandarins (3–7) are smaller trees bearing smaller fruit with orange flesh (9,11) and rind color. Mandarins have both apomictic and zygotic reproduction, and some are self-compatible. Oranges (8,10) are generally intermediate in tree and fruit size; the flesh (10) and rind color is commonly orange, and apomictic reproduction is always present. (The sour orange shown (12) is immature.)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2906/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Two wild species are believed to have contributed to domesticated pummelos, mandarins and oranges (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 1</a>). 'Pummelos' have generally been identified with the wild species <i>C. maxima</i> (Burm.) Merrill, which is indigenous to Southeast Asia, on the basis of morphology and genetic markers. Although 'mandarins' are similarly widely identified with the species <i>C. reticulata</i> Blanco<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Scora, R.W. On the history and origin of citrus. Bull. Torrey Bot. Club 102, 369–375 (1975)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d9185e1452">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Barrett, H.C. &amp; Rhodes, A.M. A numerical taxonomic study of affinity relationships in cultivated citrus and its close relatives. Syst. Bot. 1, 105–136 (1976)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d9185e1455">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Nicolosi, E. et al. Citrus phylogeny and genetic origin of important species as investigated by molecular markers. Theor. Appl. Genet. 100, 1155–1166 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d9185e1458">6</a></sup>, wild populations of <i>C. reticulata</i> have not been definitively described. Various authors have taken different approaches to classifying mandarins, and several naming conventions have been developed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Swingle, W.T. &amp; Reece, H.C. in The Citrus Industry 2nd edn, Vol. 1 (eds. Reuther, W., Webber, H.J. &amp; Batchelor, L.D.) 190–430 (University of California Press, Berkeley, California, USA, 1967)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d9185e1466">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Tanaka, T. Fundamental discussion of Citrus classification. Studia Citrologica 14, 1–6 (1977)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d9185e1469">8</a></sup>. Here we emphasize that the term 'mandarin' is a commercial or popular designation, referring to citrus with small, easily peeled, sweet fruit, but is not necessarily a taxonomic one. We use the qualifier 'traditional' to refer to mandarins without previously suspected admixture from other ancestral species, to distinguish them from mandarin types that are known or believed to be recent hybrids. For clarity, we use × in the systematic name of such known hybrids (as described in ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Moore, G.A. Oranges and lemons: clues to the taxonomy of Citrus from molecular markers. Trends Genet. 17, 536–540 (2001)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d9185e1472">9</a>). Recognizing that genome sequencing and diversity analysis have provided insights into the domestication history of several other fruit crops<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Cornille, A. et al. New insight into the history of domesticated apple: secondary contribution of the European wild apple to the genome of cultivated varieties. PLoS Genet. 8, e1002703 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d9185e1476">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Myles, S. et al. Genetic structure and domestication history of the grape. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 108, 3530–3535 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d9185e1479">11</a></sup>, cereals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Huang, X. et al. A map of rice genome variation reveals the origin of cultivated rice. Nature 490, 497–501 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d9185e1483">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Hufford, M.B. et al. Comparative population genomics of maize domestication and improvement. Nat. Genet. 44, 808–811 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d9185e1486">13</a></sup> and other crops (reviewed in ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Morrell, P.L., Buckler, E.S. &amp; Ross-Ibarra, J. Crop genomics: advances and applications. Nat. Rev. Genet. 13, 85–96 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d9185e1489">14</a>), we sequenced and analyzed the genomes of a diverse collection of cultivated pummelos, mandarins and oranges (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Table 1</a>) to test the pummelo-mandarin species hypothesis and to uncover the origins of several important citrus cultivars.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">A high-quality reference genome for citrus</h3><p>To provide a genomic platform for analyzing <i>Citrus</i>, we generated a high-quality reference genome from <span class="stix">∼</span>7× Sanger dideoxy whole-genome shotgun coverage of a haploid derivative of Clementine mandarin (<i>C.</i> × <i>clementina</i> cv. Clemenules)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Germana, M.A. et al. Cytological and molecular characterization of three gametoclones of Citrus clementina. BMC Plant Biol. 13, 129 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d9185e1517">15</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Tables 2–4</a>). The use of haploid material (derived from a single ovule after induced gynogenesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Germana, M.A. et al. Cytological and molecular characterization of three gametoclones of Citrus clementina. BMC Plant Biol. 13, 129 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d9185e1528">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Aleza, P. et al. Recovery and characterization of a Citrus clementina Hort. ex Tan. 'Clemenules' haploid plant selected to establish the reference whole Citrus genome sequence. BMC Plant Biol. 9, 110 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d9185e1531">16</a></sup>) removes complications that arise when assembling outbred diploid genomes. The resulting 301.4-Mb reference sequence is nearly complete, with superior assembly contiguity (contig L50 = 119 kb) and scaffolding (scaffold L50 before pseudochromosome construction = 6.8 Mb) compared to those of a recently published sweet-orange draft sequence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Xu, Q. et al. The draft genome of sweet orange (Citrus sinensis). Nat. Genet. 45, 59–66 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d9185e1535">17</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Table 5</a>). The long scaffolds allowed us to construct pseudochromosomes by assigning 96% of the assembly to a location on the nine citrus chromosomes by using the latest citrus genetic map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Ollitrault, P. et al. A reference genetic map of C. clementina hort. ex Tan.: citrus evolution inferences from comparative mapping. BMC Genomics 13, 593 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d9185e1545">18</a></sup>; in comparison, only 79% was assigned in the sweet-orange draft<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Xu, Q. et al. The draft genome of sweet orange (Citrus sinensis). Nat. Genet. 45, 59–66 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d9185e1550">17</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 2</a>). We also inferred the phase of the two diploid Clementine haplotypes from sequence data, identifying ten crossovers from the meiosis that produced the haploid Clementine (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>), and annotated nominal centromeres as large regions of low recombination (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Figs. 2–11</a>). We also independently sequenced and assembled a draft genome of the (diploid) sweet-orange variety 'Ridge Pineapple' by combining deep 454 sequencing with light Sanger sampling (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Tables 5–10</a>), and we inferred chromosome phasing by using the recently reported rough-draft genome of a sweet orange–derived dihaploid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Xu, Q. et al. The draft genome of sweet orange (Citrus sinensis). Nat. Genet. 45, 59–66 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d9185e1570">17</a></sup>.</p><p>The citrus genome retains substantial segmental synteny (that is, local collinearity) with other eudicots, although it has experienced extensive large-scale rearrangement on the chromosome scale (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 4</a>). We propose a specific model, based on analysis of synteny, for the origin of the citrus genome from the paleohexaploid eudicot ancestor<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Salse, J. In silico archeogenomics unveils modern plant genome organisation, regulation and evolution. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 15, 122–130 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d9185e1580">19</a></sup> through a series of chromosome fissions and fusions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Figs. 12–14</a>). Despite the compactness of the citrus genome, 45% is repetitive, with long-terminal-repeat retrotransposons and numerous uncharacterized elements, each making up nearly half of the repetitive content; the remainder comprises DNA transposons and long interspersed elements (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Table 11</a>). We identified <span class="stix">∼</span>25,000 protein-coding gene loci in both Clementine and sweet orange by computational methods combined with extensive long-read 454 and Sanger expressed-sequence-tags (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Tables 12</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">13</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Investigation of citrus ancestry</h3><p>To investigate the origin of cultivated varieties, we sequenced the genomes of four mandarins (including Clementine), two pummelos and one sour orange, as well as the sweet-orange genome reported above (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#Tab1">Table 1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Tables 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">14–16</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Notes 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">6</a>). (Cultivars derived from <i>Citrus medica</i> (the third purported wild species), i.e., citrons, limes and lemons, were not part of this study.) We aligned whole-genome shotgun reads from each cultivar to the sweet-orange chloroplast genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Bausher, M.G., Singh, N.D., Lee, S.B., Jansen, R.K. &amp; Daniell, H. The complete chloroplast genome sequence of Citrus sinensis (L.) Osbeck var 'Ridge Pineapple': organization and phylogenetic relationships to other angiosperms. BMC Plant Biol. 6, 21 (2006)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d9185e1630">37</a></sup> and identified high-quality single-nucleotide variants (SNVs) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 6</a>). We excluded indels and larger structural variants from this analysis. We readily identified two distinct types of chloroplast genomes (cpDNA), with mandarins all having one type (which we define as M for mandarin or <i>C. reticulata</i>) and pummelos and oranges sharing another type (defined as P for pummelo or <i>C. maxima</i>, with limited variation within each cpDNA type (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 15</a>), in agreement with prior studies of mitochondrial markers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Froelicher, Y. et al. New universal mitochondrial PCR markers reveal new information on maternal citrus phylogeny. Tree Genet. Genomes 7, 49–61 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d9185e1650">20</a></sup>. Citrus nuclear genomes tell a more complex story (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Notes 7–9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Tables 17–19</a>). By aligning whole-genome shotgun reads to the haploid Clementine reference and identifying high-quality SNVs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 6</a>), we found that although the sequenced pummelos are evidently genotypes from the sexual <i>C. maxima</i> species with minimal introgression of other species, all the mandarin-type citrus that we sequenced show substantial admixture with pummelo and therefore cannot simply be selections from an ancestral <i>C. reticulata</i> population (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#Fig2">Figs. 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#Fig3">3</a>). The sweet and sour oranges are also hybrids of varying complexity, with pummelo-type chloroplast genomes in both cases.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Sequenced cultivars and proportions derived from the ancestral species <i>C. reticulata</i> and <i>C. maxima</i></b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nbt.2906/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Nucleotide-diversity distribution in citrus."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Nucleotide-diversity distribution in citrus.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2906/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2906/MediaObjects/41587_2014_Article_BFnbt2906_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2906/MediaObjects/41587_2014_Article_BFnbt2906_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="777"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Nucleotide-heterozygosity distribution computed in overlapping 100-kb windows (with 5-kb step size) across the low-acid (LAP) and Chandler (CHP) pummelo genomes and between the nonshared haplotypes of this parent-child pair (LAP/CHP). The peak at <span class="stix">∼</span>6 heterozygous sites/kb in all three pairwise comparisons represents the characteristic nucleotide diversity of the species <i>C. maxima</i>; the peak near <span class="stix">∼</span>1 heterozygous site/kb reflects a bottleneck in the ancestral <i>C. maxima</i> population after divergence from <i>C. reticulata</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 10</a>). (<b>b</b>) Nucleotide heterozygosity for the traditional Willowleaf mandarin (WLM) plotted along chromosome 6, computed in overlapping windows of 200 kb (with 100-kb step size). This chromosome shows an example of the clear discontinuity in single-nucleotide-variant heterozygosity levels between <span class="stix">∼</span>5/kb in the M/M segment (orange bar) and <span class="stix">∼</span>17/kb in the M/P segment (blue bar). (<b>c</b>) Nucleotide heterozygosity distribution computed in overlapping 500-kb windows (with 5-kb step size) in Ponkan (PKM, solid line) and Willowleaf (WLM, dashed line) mandarins. Genomic segments are designated M/M, M/P or P/P on the basis of a set of 1,537,264 SNPs that differentiate <i>C. reticulata</i> (M) from <i>C. maxima</i> (P). Both mandarins contain admixed segments from <i>C. maxima</i> introgression (M/P) as well as M/M segments, and these are plotted and normalized separately for easy comparison. (<b>d</b>) Nucleotide heterozygosity distribution computed in overlapping windows of 500 kb (5-kb offsets) for sweet orange (SWO) and sour orange (SSO). The three different genotypes of the sweet-orange genome (M/M, P/P and M/P) and the sour-orange genotype M/P are normalized and plotted separately.</p><p>
                          <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM12">Source data</a>
                        </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2906/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Admixture patterns and nucleotide diversity in cultivated citrus."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Admixture patterns and nucleotide diversity in cultivated citrus.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2906/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2906/MediaObjects/41587_2014_Article_BFnbt2906_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2906/MediaObjects/41587_2014_Article_BFnbt2906_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="739"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>For each of the three groups of sequenced citrus, variation in nucleotide diversity (averaged over 500-kb windows with step size 250 kb) is shown across the genome for one representative cultivar above genotype maps (horizontal bars). Green, <i>C. maxima/C. maxima</i>; blue, <i>C. maxima/C. reticulata</i>; orange, <i>C. reticulata/C. reticulata</i>; gray, unknown. The nine chromosomes are numbered at top. (<b>a</b>) Sweet orange (SWO) nucleotide diversity with genotype maps for sweet orange and sour orange (SSO), indicating the <i>C. maxima/C. maxima</i> genotype (green segments present on chromosomes 2 and 8) in sweet orange. (<b>b</b>) Willowleaf mandarin (WLM) nucleotide diversity and genotype maps for three traditional mandarins (Ponkan mandarin (PKM), Willowleaf mandarin (WLM) and Huanglingmiao (HLM)) and three recent mandarin types (Clementine (CLM), W. Murcott mandarin (WMM) and haploid Clementine reference (HCR)). For the haploid Clementine reference sequence, orange and green segments indicate <i>C. reticulata</i> and <i>C. maxima</i> haplotypes, respectively. All five mandarin types show pummelo introgressions (blue or green segments). (<b>c</b>) Low-acid pummelo (LAP) nucleotide diversity and genotype maps for two pummelos (low-acid pummelo and Chandler pummelo (CHP)).</p><p>
                          <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM13">Source data</a>
                        </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2906/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Ancestry of pummelos</h3><p>The two diploid pummelos that we sequenced contain three distinct haplotypes, because low-acid (Siamese Sweet) pummelo is the known female parent of Chandler pummelo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Cameron, J.W.S. R K Chandler: an early-ripening hybrid pummelo derived from a low-acid parent. Hilgardia 30, 359–364 (1961)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d9185e2479">21</a></sup>, so that the two pummelos share one haplotype at each locus (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 16</a>). Within the two sequenced pummelos and between their nonshared alleles (derived from the other parent of Chandler, i.e., Siamese Pink pummelo) we observed modest levels of heterozygosity, with a genome-wide nucleotide heterozygosity of 5.7 heterozygous (het) sites/kb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>). The presence of a second low-heterozygosity peak (<span class="stix">∼</span>1 het site/kb) in the distribution can be explained by a strong ancient bottleneck in the <i>C. maxima</i> population <span class="stix">∼</span>100,000–300,000 years ago (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 10</a>). Our reanalysis of three Chinese pummelos previously reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Xu, Q. et al. The draft genome of sweet orange (Citrus sinensis). Nat. Genet. 45, 59–66 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d9185e2499">17</a></sup> (including the Wusuan pummelo, which we identify as coming from the same somatic lineage as Siamese Sweet pummelo) shows that both Thai and Chinese pummelos are derived from the same wild population (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 11</a>). Only a single short 1.5-Mb segment on chromosome 2 of Chandler shows unusually high heterozygosity that could reflect interspecific introgression (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 17</a>). These observations are consistent with pummelo domestication by selection from a wild sexual <i>C. maxima</i> population.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Ancestry of mandarins</h3><p>The four mandarin genomes that we sequenced included a range of mandarin types: two traditional mandarins without prior suspected admixture (Ponkan, an old and widely grown Asian variety that was presumed to be typical of <i>C. reticulata</i>, and Willowleaf, a common Mediterranean variety) as well as two mandarins believed to be hybrids of traditional mandarins with other citrus (Clementine, the diploid parent of the haploid reference accession, and W. Murcott, believed to be synonymous with the cultivar also known as Nadorcott and Afourer and widely grown in California and the Mediterranean (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 1</a>)). In contrast to those of pummelos, the mandarin accessions that we sequenced typically include segments of high nucleotide heterozygosity (<span class="stix">∼</span>17 het sites/kb, consistently with interspecific variation) that span tens of centimorgans or megabase pairs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 18</a>). These highly heterozygous blocks are interspersed with long segments of substantially lower levels of heterozygosity (<span class="stix">∼</span>5 het sites/kb) that are consistent with intraspecific variation and are clearly distinct from the higher-heterozygosity blocks (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>). In the lower-heterozygosity segments, both alleles are often distinct from those observed in the pummelos and presumably derive from <i>C. reticulata</i>, which is widely cited as the true species from which cultivated mandarins arose<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Swingle, W.T. &amp; Reece, H.C. in The Citrus Industry 2nd edn, Vol. 1 (eds. Reuther, W., Webber, H.J. &amp; Batchelor, L.D.) 190–430 (University of California Press, Berkeley, California, USA, 1967)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d9185e2539">7</a></sup>. In contrast, we found that the higher-heterozygosity blocks typically carry one allele that matches the pummelos and one nonpummelo allele, also presumably <i>C. reticulata</i>. The presumptive <i>C. reticulata</i> alleles are typically common to multiple mandarin accessions, thus further supporting their identification.</p><p>Our surprising conclusion is that traditional mandarin types, such as Ponkan and Willowleaf, are in fact interspecific introgressions of <i>C. maxima</i> (pummelo) into <i>C. reticulata</i> (wild mandarin). Furthermore, although these traditional mandarins were previously thought to be unrelated, we detected extensive haplotype sharing between them (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Figs. 19</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">20</a>). Because microsatellite-based population structure analyses of a wide range of citrus genotypes show mandarins as a defined cluster of genotypes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Barkley, N.A., Roose, M.L., Krueger, R.R. &amp; Federici, C.T. Assessing genetic diversity and population structure in a citrus germplasm collection utilizing simple sequence repeat markers (SSRs). Theor. Appl. Genet. 112, 1519–1531 (2006)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d9185e2568">22</a></sup>, such admixture is probably widespread among mandarin types. Indeed, reanalysis of a recently sequenced Chinese mandarin<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Xu, Q. et al. The draft genome of sweet orange (Citrus sinensis). Nat. Genet. 45, 59–66 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d9185e2572">17</a></sup> in the light of our discovery of interspecific introgression in multiple mandarin types, shows that the traditional Chinese Huanglingmiao mandarin (incorrectly treated previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Aleza, P. et al. Recovery and characterization of a Citrus clementina Hort. ex Tan. 'Clemenules' haploid plant selected to establish the reference whole Citrus genome sequence. BMC Plant Biol. 9, 110 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d9185e2576">16</a></sup> as a pure <i>C. reticulata</i>) also exhibits unsuspected admixture between <i>C. reticulata</i> and <i>C. maxima</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 11</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Fig. 3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 21</a>).</p><p>Although none of our cultivated mandarin genotypes represent pure <i>C. reticulata</i>, we can nevertheless extract wild mandarin alleles from our data by comparing the (admixed) cultivated mandarins with each other and with the two pure pummelos. By such genome-wide comparisons, we identified 1,537,264 putative fixed single-nucleotide differences between <i>C. reticulata</i> and <i>C. maxima</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 22</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Data Set 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 7</a>). These diagnostic variants can in turn be used to partition the mandarin, pummelo and orange genomes into segments according to their species ancestry (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#Fig3">Fig. 3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 23</a>). The characterization of <i>C. reticulata</i> genomic segments from modern mandarins is analogous to the extraction of African haplotypes from Mexican Americans<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Johnson, N.A. et al. Ancestral components of admixed genomes in a Mexican cohort. PLoS Genet. 7, e1002410 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d9185e2630">23</a></sup> and Native American haplotypes from extant ethnic human populations that are admixtures with American, African and European roots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Bustamante, C.D., Burchard, E.G. &amp; De la Vega, F.M. Genomics for the world. Nature 475, 163–165 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d9185e2634">24</a></sup>.</p><p>We can estimate the parameters of a simple population-genetic model for the divergence of <i>C. reticulata</i> and <i>C. maxima</i> from an ancestral South Asian citrus founder population, using a coalescent framework and our collection of fixed interspecific differences and intraspecific variation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Figs. 24–26</a>). This analysis is consistent with effective population sizes of several hundred thousand trees for <i>C. maxima</i> and somewhat fewer for <i>C. reticulata</i>, with a larger effective population size for pummelos, in keeping with their higher heterozygosity. The likely occurrence of apomixis in wild mandarin populations, a trait that seems to be absent in <i>C. maxima</i>, may contribute to reducing the effective <i>C. reticulata</i> population size relative to the census size. If we assume a per-site mutation rate (μ) of <span class="stix">∼</span>1–2 × 10<sup>−9</sup>/y (comparable to that observed in poplar trees<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Tuskan, G.A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596–1604 (2006)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d9185e2668">25</a></sup>), then we can estimate that <i>C. reticulata</i> and <i>C. maxima</i> diverged <span class="stix">∼</span>1.6–3.2 Myr ago; this is consistent with the divergence between <i>Citrus</i> and the related genus <i>Poncirus</i>, which is estimated at 4–9.6 Myr ago (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Pfeil, B.E. &amp; Crisp, M.D. The age and biogeography of Citrus and the orange subfamily (Rutaceae: Aurantioideae) in Australasia and New Caledonia. Am. J. Bot. 95, 1621–1631 (2008)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d9185e2684">26</a>). As noted, the excess of low-heterozygosity segments in pummelo is consistent with a substantial population bottleneck several hundred thousand years ago and before the separation of Thai and Chinese pummelo lineages (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Notes 9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">11</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 27</a>).</p><p>Some specific citrus genotypes are generally recognized as hybrid varieties. For example, Clementine mandarin (also known as Algerian tangerine) is believed to be a chance seedling from a Mediterranean mandarin (e.g., Willowleaf) selected just over a century ago in Algeria<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Trabut, J.L. L'hybridation des Citrus: une nouvelle tangérine 'la Clémentine'. Revue Horticole 10, 232–234 (1902)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d9185e2702">27</a></sup>. Although various male parents have been proposed, serological and molecular studies demonstrated that the Clementine was likely to be a hybrid of mandarin and sweet orange<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Nicolosi, E. et al. Citrus phylogeny and genetic origin of important species as investigated by molecular markers. Theor. Appl. Genet. 100, 1155–1166 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d9185e2706">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Ollitrault, P. et al. A reference genetic map of C. clementina hort. ex Tan.: citrus evolution inferences from comparative mapping. BMC Genomics 13, 593 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d9185e2709">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Samaan, L.G. Studies on the origin of Clementine tangerine (Citrus reticulata Blanco). Euphytica 31, 167–173 (1982)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d9185e2712">28</a></sup>. We confirm this hypothesis at the sequence level by definitively identifying a Willowleaf and sweet-orange allele at each Clementine locus; by demarcating the recombination breakpoints in the meiosis that produced the haploid Clementine sequence; and by determining the Willowleaf and sweet-orange haplotypes that contributed to diploid Clementine (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Figs. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">28–31</a>). Similarly, the W. Murcott mandarin is believed to be a chance zygotic seedling of Murcott tangor, itself a presumed F1 hybrid of sweet orange and an unknown mandarin. Our sequence analysis is consistent with the suspected grandparent-grandchild relationship between sweet orange and W. Murcott (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 10</a>). Although the other parent and grandparent of W. Murcott are not known, a search for these ancestors will be enabled by the other observed alleles.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Ancestry of oranges</h3><p>Sweet orange (<i>Citrus</i> × <i>sinensis</i> L. Osbeck) is the citrus type most widely cultivated for fruit and juice and is widely believed to be an interspecific hybrid, but its origin is unknown<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Scora, R.W. On the history and origin of citrus. Bull. Torrey Bot. Club 102, 369–375 (1975)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d9185e2744">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Nicolosi, E. et al. Citrus phylogeny and genetic origin of important species as investigated by molecular markers. Theor. Appl. Genet. 100, 1155–1166 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d9185e2747">6</a></sup>. Different sweet-orange cultivars share the same genomic organization with little sequence variation, having arisen by mutation from the original sweet-orange domesticate (as described, for example, in ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Novelli, V.M., Cristofani, M., Souza, A.A. &amp; Machado, M.A. Development and characterization of polymorphic microsatellite markers for the sweet orange (Citrus sinensis L. Osbeck). Genet. Mol. Biol. 29, 90–96 (2006)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d9185e2750">29</a>). Using our genome-wide catalog of fixed <i>C. reticulata</i> versus <i>C. maxima</i> alleles, we can represent the sweet-orange genome as segments of these two parental species or hybrid segments thereof (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#Fig2">Fig. 2d</a>), with clear boundaries between different segment types (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>). A recently proposed (P × M) × M backcross scheme for the derivation of sweet orange from mandarin and pummelo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Xu, Q. et al. The draft genome of sweet orange (Citrus sinensis). Nat. Genet. 45, 59–66 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d9185e2770">17</a></sup>, however, is easily ruled out by the presence of clear P/P (i.e., <i>C. maxima</i>/<i>C. maxima</i>) segments in sweet orange, which would require both parents to have some pummelo ancestry. (The P/P segment on chromosome 2 has been confirmed by directed resequencing of three genes in this region<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Garcia-Lor, A. et al. A nuclear phylogenetic analysis: SNPs, indels and SSRs deliver new insights into the relationships in the 'true citrus fruit trees' group (Citrinae, Rutaceae) and the origin of cultivated species. Ann. Bot. 111, 1–19 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d9185e2781">30</a></sup>.)</p><p>Unexpectedly, in our analysis we found that sweet orange shares alleles with Ponkan mandarin across nearly three-quarters of the genome, and many of the same segments are also shared with Willowleaf and Huanglingmiao (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 32</a>). This leads to the surprising conclusion that these three traditional mandarins, previously considered to be independent selections, in fact show substantial kinship with each other and with an ancestor of sweet orange, thus suggesting much more limited genetic diversity among the traditional mandarins than has previously been recognized (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 10</a>). The nature of the other parent of sweet orange is more difficult to infer, but the distribution of heterozygous segments in sweet orange (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 33</a>) and its pummelo-type chloroplast genome would be more readily accounted for if the female parent were itself a pummelo with substantial introgression of wild mandarin (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 9</a>).</p><p>Finally, Seville or sour orange (also known as <i>Citrus</i> × <i>aurantium</i>), which has historically been an important rootstock for citrus and, more familiarly, is used in marmalade and other products, is another traditional cultivar type that is widely regarded as a pummelo-mandarin hybrid. Our genomic analysis shows that sour orange is indeed the direct result of a simple interspecific F1 cross between a pummelo (<i>C. maxima</i>) seed parent and a wild-mandarin (<i>C. reticulata</i>) pollen parent (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 34</a>). Surprisingly in light of our discovery of widespread pummelo admixture among traditional mandarins, no such admixture is found in the <i>C. reticulata</i> parent of sour orange, but the specific parental genotypes remain unknown. Sour orange may have arisen as a natural hybrid of two wild <i>Citrus</i> species and persisted by virtue of its reproduction through apomixis and subsequent deliberate human cultivation and distribution. We found no detectable recent relationship between sweet and sour orange.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Chinese Mangshan is a distinct species, <i>C. mangshanensis</i></h3><p>Among cultivars traditionally classified as mandarins, however, we found another surprise. Our analysis of the genome of a presumed wild mandarin from Mangshan, China<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Xu, Q. et al. The draft genome of sweet orange (Citrus sinensis). Nat. Genet. 45, 59–66 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d9185e2842">17</a></sup> (CMS) shows (i) a chloroplast genome distinct from that of both <i>C. reticulata</i> and <i>C. maxima</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>); (ii) limited heterozygosity (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>), again uniformly distributed across the genome, with no segments of pummelo or mandarin ancestry, thus indicating no admixture; and (iii) <span class="stix">∼</span>2% homozygous differences from both <i>C. reticulata</i> and <i>C. maxima</i> uniformly across the genome, a rate comparable to the divergence between <i>C. maxima</i> and <i>C. reticulata</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>). At the level of nucleotide diversity, CMS is as diverged from <i>C. maxima</i> and <i>C. reticulata</i> as <i>C. maxima</i> and <i>C. reticulata</i> are from each other (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>), and it is clearly separated from pummelos, oranges and mandarins by principal coordinate analysis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#Fig4">Fig. 4c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 11</a>). By all these measures, we find that Mangshan mandarin is unrelated to the other cultivated mandarins discussed above (including Huanglingmiao mandarin). We therefore propose that, despite its morphology, Mangshan mandarin represents a distinct species from <i>C. reticulata</i>, supporting the nomenclature <i>C. mangshanensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Liu, G.F., He, S.W. &amp; Li, W.B. Two new species of citrus in China. Acta Botanica Yunnanica 12, 287–289 (1990)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d9185e2902">31</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Mangshan mandarin is a species distinct from C. maxima and C. reticulata."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Mangshan mandarin is a species distinct from <i>C. maxima</i> and <i>C. reticulata</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2906/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2906/MediaObjects/41587_2014_Article_BFnbt2906_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2906/MediaObjects/41587_2014_Article_BFnbt2906_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="566"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Midpoint-rooted neighbor-joining phylogenetic tree of citrus chloroplast genomes. (<b>b</b>) Frequency distributions of the pairwise sequence divergences (across 100-kb windows) between Mangshan mandarin (CMS) and <i>C. maxima</i> (green), CMS and <i>C. reticulata</i> (orange), <i>C. reticulata</i> and <i>C. maxima</i> (light blue) as well as the distinctly lower CMS intrinsic nucleotide diversity (dashed blue). <i>Ret</i>, <i>C. reticulata</i>; <i>max</i>, <i>C. maxima</i>; het, heterozygous. (<b>c</b>) The first two coordinates of principal coordinate analysis of the citrus nuclear genomes, based on pairwise distances and metric multidimensional scaling. The <i>C. maxima</i>–<i>C. reticulata</i> axis (principal coordinate 1, 47.5% variance) separates pummelos (green) from mandarins (orange), with oranges (blue) lying in between; principal coordinate 2 (19.6% of variance) separates CMS (purple) from the others.</p><p>
                          <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM14">Source data</a>
                        </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2906/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>Our genomic analyses clarify some of the murky early history of citrus domestication. The nuclear and chloroplast genomes of cultivated pummelos are consistent with the identification of pummelos as a single <i>Citrus</i> species, <i>C. maxima</i>. In contrast, the nuclear genomes of sequenced mandarin-type cultivars all contain substantial admixture of <i>C. maxima</i>, despite the similarity of mandarin chloroplast sequences. Our results thus show that the various conventional <i>Citrus</i> taxonomies that associate mandarin citrus types with the ancestral <i>Citrus</i> species <i>C. reticulata</i> are too simplistic. It is particularly surprising that even the traditional mandarin types with no prior suspicion of relatedness or admixture, such as Ponkan, Willowleaf and Huanglingmiao mandarin, show substantial haplotype sharing and all include introgressed pummelo segments. A supposed wild mandarin from Mangshan, China turns out to represent a distinct taxon only distantly related to <i>C. reticulata</i>, on the basis of analysis of its nuclear and chloroplast genomes. (In a previous analysis of sweet-orange ancestry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Xu, Q. et al. The draft genome of sweet orange (Citrus sinensis). Nat. Genet. 45, 59–66 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d9185e3011">17</a></sup>, Mangshan mandarin Clementine and Huanglingmiao were used to represent <i>C. reticulata.</i> Our discovery of substantial pummelo admixture in Clementine and Huanglingmiao, and the distinctness of Mangshan mandarin from <i>C. reticulata,</i> further invalidate the conclusions in ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Xu, Q. et al. The draft genome of sweet orange (Citrus sinensis). Nat. Genet. 45, 59–66 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d9185e3020">17</a>.)</p><p>Remarkably, even in the absence of a pure type specimen for <i>C. reticulata</i>, we can characterize the genome of this wild mandarin progenitor species from genome-wide comparative analysis of admixed descendants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Johnson, N.A. et al. Ancestral components of admixed genomes in a Mexican cohort. PLoS Genet. 7, e1002410 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d9185e3030">23</a></sup>. Our collection of 1,537,264 SNPs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Data Set 1</a>) that differentiate <i>C. reticulata</i> from <i>C. maxima</i> can be used to guide the search for pure <i>C. reticulata</i> mandarin types (or to recognize other cryptic species) among the hundreds of known cultivars and other germplasm accessions. Small-fruited mandarins that are less desirable for fresh consumption on the basis of appearance, flavor, texture and aroma may be considered likely candidates. With the discovery that <i>C. mangshanensis</i> is a distinct group, the possibility of additional yet-undescribed wild <i>Citrus</i> species must also be considered.</p><p>The prevalence of interspecific admixture in cultivated citrus suggests that either early in domestication or in a natural hybrid zone before domestication, <i>C. reticulata</i> and <i>C. maxima</i> interbreeding occurred. Given the typical size of the hybrid blocks, only a few generations of introgression occurred before the selection of attractive cultivars, which were then propagated asexually by apomictic or vegetative means, perhaps in southern China<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Gmitter, F.G. &amp; Hu, X. The possible role of Yunnan, China, in the origin of contemporary citrus species (Rutaceae). Econ. Bot. 44, 267–277 (1990)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d9185e3062">32</a></sup>. Our analysis of sweet orange and sour orange shows that these ancient and widely cultivated genotypes are pummelo-mandarin admixtures that are unrelated to each other, despite some degree of phenotypic similarity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Morton, J.F. Fruits of Warm Climates (Florida Flair Books, Miami, 1987)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d9185e3066">33</a></sup>. The discovery that sour orange is a simple F1 hybrid of <i>C. maxima</i> and <i>C. reticulata</i> implies that pure <i>C. reticulata</i> individuals were part of the breeding germplasm at the origin of sour orange. Remarkably, we found that extant Ponkan, Willowleaf and Huanglingmiao mandarins are related to each other and to the male parent of sweet orange. Although the female parent of sweet orange remains unknown, it cannot have been a pure pummelo (though it had pummelo cytoplasm, on the basis of cpDNA and mitochondrial DNA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Froelicher, Y. et al. New universal mitochondrial PCR markers reveal new information on maternal citrus phylogeny. Tree Genet. Genomes 7, 49–61 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d9185e3080">20</a></sup>). Its identity is constrained by the high proportion of hybrid P/M segments in sweet orange, which could be naturally explained if the female parent of sweet orange were (P × M) × P.</p><p>Like many other agricultural enterprises, the global citrus industry relies substantially on large-scale monoculture, and this makes it particularly challenging to meet consumer demand for greater product diversity while trying to incorporate tolerance and/or resistance to biotic and potentially catastrophic abiotic stresses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Gottwald, T.R. Current epidemiological understanding of citrus Huanglongbing. Annu. Rev. Phytopathol. 48, 119–139 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d9185e3087">34</a></sup>. Advances in citrus genomics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Talon, M. &amp; Gmitter, F.G. Jr. Citrus genomics. Int. J. Plant Genomics 2008, 528361 (2008)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d9185e3091">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Gmitter, F.G. et al. Citrus genomics. Tree Genet. Genomes 8, 611–626 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.2906#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d9185e3094">36</a></sup> should soon allow the identification of the somatic mutations that, with their ancient genetic backgrounds, underlie the diversity of citrus color, flavor and aroma in modern cultivars. Our analysis of the relationships between cultivated citrus and the ancestral species from which they were derived emphasizes the limited ancestral germplasm that contributed to the commercially important cultivar types, such as sweet orange, and highlights the opportunities for the creation of new combinations of the ancestral citrus types with new fruit quality traits or even the re-creation of sweet orange with improved disease resistance via sexual hybridization, beyond the current approaches based on somatic mutations and genetic engineering.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Haploid <i>C.</i> × <i>clementina</i> 'Clemenules' sequencing and assembly.</h3><p>A total of 4.6 million Sanger reads (including 469,000 fosmid-end and 73,000 BAC-end reads), were obtained from an induced haploid plant <i>C.</i> × <i>clementina</i> 'Clemenules', assembled with Arachne and integrated with a genetic map producing chromosome-scale pseudomolecules (nearly 97% of ESTs aligned to the genome) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12"><i>C. × sinensis</i> genome sequencing and assembly.</h3><p>A total of 16.5 Gb sequence (36 million 454 reads and 750,000 Sanger PE reads) was generated from <i>C.</i> × <i>sinensis</i> 'Ridge Pineapple' and assembled with Newbler (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 3</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Annotation of repeats and genes in citrus genome assemblies.</h3><p>Repeat analysis was performed separately in the Clementine and sweet-orange genomes. The method used RepeatModeler to find new repeats in the genome sequence, which were masked with RepeatMasker. Following this, PASA was used to align and assemble ESTs (1.6 million for Clementine; 6.5 million for sweet orange) and integrate Fgenesh+, exonerate and GenomeScan gene predictions to generate gene models (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 4</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Evolutionary comparisons with other plant genomes.</h3><p>Evolutionary comparisons to plant genomes used ortholog assignment to generate chromosome-to-chromosome relationships within and between genomes and predict ancestral genome structures (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 5</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Analysis of resequencing data sets.</h3><p>Illumina shotgun sequence reads from eight accessions (17 × −110 × depth; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#Tab1">Table 1</a>) were mapped to the haploid Clementine reference with bwa, and single-nucleotide variants were identified with SAMtools and in-house scripts (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 6</a>). Heterozygosity in diploid accessions was estimated in windows of 100–500 kb by division of the number of confidently inferred heterozygous single nucleotide variant ('het') sites by the number of eligible sites in the window at which confident variant calls could be made, on the basis of depth and alignment quality (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 6</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Identification of two ancestral species (<i>C. maxima</i> versus <i>C. reticulata</i> alleles) and admixture analysis.</h3><p>Diagnostic alleles for the two ancestral <i>Citrus</i> species, <i>C. maxima</i> and <i>C. reticulata,</i> were derived from a comparative analysis of two pummelos and two traditional mandarin types and were used to study the admixture patterns in the sequenced cultivars (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Notes 7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">8</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Population genetic analysis and simulations.</h3><p>Population genetic analysis of the two citrus species and demographic inference were based on coalescent simulations conducted with MaCS (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 10</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Analysis of relatedness in citrus.</h3><p>Parentage and relatedness analysis for Clementine and other citrus genomes made use of homozygous SNPs in each diploid genome relative to the haploid Clementine reference as well as to the inferred second haplotype of Clementine (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Notes 9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">11</a>). In the same way, the haploid sweet-orange assembly was used for identifying shared haplotypes with sweet orange (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 9</a>). A modified identical-by-state (IBS) method was used for haplotype-sharing analysis among mandarins and other citrus pairs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2906#MOESM15">Supplementary Note 9</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Accession codes.</h3><p>The reference haploid Clementine assembly and annotation has been deposited in the NCBI genome database under accession code <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/genome/?term=AMZM00000000">AMZM00000000</a>. Sanger whole-genome sequencing for Clementine has been deposited in the NCBI trace archive under <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/Traces/trace.cgi?%26cmd=retrieve%26val=SPECIES_CODE='CITRUSCLEMENTINA'%26retrieve=Submit">SPECIES_CODE='CITRUS CLEMENTINA'</a>. This Whole-genome shotgun project has been deposited at DDBJ/EMBL/GenBank under accession code <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=JJOQ00000000">JJOQ00000000</a>. Whole-genome sequencing data for sweet orange have been deposited in the NCBI Sequence Read Archive under BioProject <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA225968">PRJNA225968</a> (454-sequencing data) and in the NCBI trace archive under <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/Traces/trace.cgi?%26=retrieve%26=SPECIES_CODE'CITRUS%20SINENSIS'%20and%20CENTER_NAME%20%3D%20'JGI'%26retrieve=Submit">SPECIES_CODE='CITRUS SINENSIS' AND CENTER_NAME ='JGI'</a>. Citrus resequencing data have been deposited in the NCBI Sequence Read Archive under the following accession codes: <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRX372786">SRX372786</a> (sour orange), <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRX372703">SRX372703</a> (sweet orange), <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRX372702">SRX372702</a> (low-acid pummelo), <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRX372688">SRX372688</a> (Chandler pummelo), <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRX372685">SRX372685</a> (Willowleaf mandarin), <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRX372687">SRX372687</a> (W. Murcott mandarin), <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRX372665">SRX372665</a> (Ponkan mandarin) and <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRX371962">SRX371962</a> (Clementine mandarin).</p></div></div></section>
